\nThanks","acceptedAnswer":{"@type":"Answer","text":"The orthogonal complement is a subspace of vectors where all of the vectors in it are orthogonal to all of the vectors in a particular subspace. For instance, if you are given a plane in , then the orthogonal complement of that plane is the line that is normal to the plane and that passes through (0,0,0). \n\nThe orthogonal component, on the other hand, is a component of a vector. Any vector in  can be written in one unique way as a sum of one vector in the plane and and one vector in the orthogonal complement of the plane. The latter vector is the orthogonal component. \n\nThe Gram-Schmidt Process is not the process of getting the orthogonal complement of a subspace from the original subspace. It is actually a method of creating an orthonormal coordinate system. For more information about orthonomal bases, Sal explains it at https://www.khanacademy.org/math/linear-algebra/alternate_bases/orthonormal_basis/v/linear-algebra-introduction-to-orthonormal-bases. \n\nThe 3rd question is not correct, but I'll answer this question anyway. A null space is the set of vectors which when multiplied by a matrix gives the 0 vector."}},{"@type":"Question","name":"What's the &quot;a member of&quot; sign Sal uses at 19:25 and, again, about 10 seconds later. It looks like a smaller-or-equal sign. Is this a common thing?","acceptedAnswer":{"@type":"Answer","text":"This is the notation for saying that the one set is a subset of another set, different from saying a single object is a member of a set. This notation is common, yes. This is a short textbook section on definition of a set and the usual notation: http://linear.ups.edu/html/section-SET.html"}},{"@type":"Question","name":"what does orthogonal mean","acceptedAnswer":{"@type":"Answer","text":"orthogonal:\n2. Math.\nd. Of two vectors or functions: perpendicular; having an inner product [dot product] equal to zero. \n\n- from Greek orthogonios &quot;right-angled,&quot; from ortho- &quot;straight&quot; + gonia &quot;angle&quot;.\n\nNotice that a zero vector is always orthogonal to any vector in the same vector space (its dot product with any other vector is 0), but not at right angles (perpendicular). (So if 2 vectors are perpendicular, they are orthogonal, but if they are orthogonal, they aren't necessarily perpendicular.)"}},{"@type":"Question","name":"I'm completely lost, why does he constantly say everything must be transposed?!\nFor example, if I got a matrix like this\n[a b c]\n[d e f]\n[g h i]\n\nthe null space is always going to be of the transposed:\n[a d g]\n[b e f]\n[c f i]\n\nwhy??","acceptedAnswer":{"@type":"Answer","text":"I don't really know why he says that. Just pay attention to which vectors are row vectors and which are column vectors. The null space of A is just all the vectors x that satisfy &quot;Ax=0&quot;, it's not the transpose of A.  \n\nIf a vector &quot;c&quot; is a row vector &quot;[c1 c2 ... cn]&quot; in R^n, then it's a 1xn matrix. If it's a column vector in R^n, then it's a nx1 matrix. If it's just a vector &quot;(c1, c2,..., cn)&quot; in R^n, then it's not a matrix, and it doesn't make sense to transpose it.\n\nThe transpose of a (row or column) vector &quot;d&quot; can be either a column or row vector, depending on whether &quot;d&quot; is a row or a column vector. Any (row or column) vector is the transpose of its transpose, and so is a transpose of some other vector. There is no need to call row vectors transposes, as Sal does here, and for me it's confusing (and I think confused) and a distraction. Calling the first row of A &quot;r1&quot; is good enough, because the letter &quot;r&quot; suggests &quot;row vector&quot;."}}]}</script><ul><li class="_1glufx42" data-test-id="discussion-list-item"><div class="_xu2jcg" data-test-id="discussion-post"><div class="_1t544yo9" tabindex="-1"><div class="_iat579e"><span class=""><a aria-label="orange juice squid orange style avatar for user Stephen Peringer Go to Stephen Peringer's user profile." class="_dwmetq" href="/profile/kaid_851381432533605900150594/discussion"><div class="_1732g80e"><img alt="orange juice squid orange style avatar for user Stephen Peringer" class="_9phhti" src="https://cdn.kastatic.org/images/avatars/svg/orange-juice-squid.svg"/></div></a></span></div><div class="_1beotn9c"><div class="_k21kjmx"><div class="_xu2jcg"><div class="_70bdamm"><div><a aria-label="Go to Stephen Peringer's user profile." class="_dwmetq" href="/profile/kaid_851381432533605900150594/discussion"><span class="_1juyo7q2">Stephen Peringer</span></a></div><a class="_1g8isxy8" href="/math/linear-algebra/alternate-bases/othogonal-complements/v/linear-algebra-orthogonal-complements?qa_expand_key=ag5zfmtoYW4tYWNhZGVteXJqCxIIVXNlckRhdGEiTHVzZXJfaWRfa2V5X2h0dHA6Ly9ub3VzZXJpZC5raGFuYWNhZGVteS5vcmcvMDIxYTYyYzU5MDY5ZTVjY2Q3NzJjYzQ3Y2FhM2FlZmUMCxIIRmVlZGJhY2sYyd8CDA&amp;qa_expand_type=question"><span class="_1eypurfx"><div aria-hidden="true" class="_xu2jcg">11 years ago</div><div class="_s217esa">Posted 11 years ago. href="/math/linear-algebra/alternate-bases/othogonal-complements/v/linear-algebra-orthogonal-complements?qa_expand_key=ag5zfmtoYW4tYWNhZGVteXJkCxIIVXNlckRhdGEiQXVzZXJfaWRfa2V5X2h0dHA6Ly9nb29nbGVpZC5raGFuYWNhZGVteS5vcmcvMTA1MzYwNTc2NjgwMTMxMTI2NjkyDAsSCEZlZWRiYWNrGICAgLDQxaYKDA&amp;qa_expand_type=answer"><span class="_1eypurfx"><div aria-hidden="true" class="_xu2jcg">7 years ago</div><div class="_s217esa">Posted 7 years ago. Direct link to Tejas's post The orthogonal complement...</div></span></a><div aria-live="polite" class="_s217esa" role="status"></div></div></div><div class="_4ebu80"><span aria-atomic="true" aria-live="polite" aria-relevant="additions text" class="_19bpjuy" data-test-id="dropdown-live-region"></span><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="menu" class="_19klbmsr" disabled="" type="button"><div class="_xu2jcg"><span class="_133hxe5q">more</span></div><div aria-hidden="true" class="_akfsuo"></div><svg class="_aeiopc" height="16" viewbox="0 0 16 16" width="16"><path d="M8 8.586l3.293-3.293a1 1 0 0 1 1.414 1.414l-4 4a1 1 0 0 1-1.414 0l-4-4a1 1 0 0 1 1.414-1.414L8 8.586z" fill="currentColor"></path></svg></button></div></div><div class="_1nza63bj"><span class="_1glfes6x"><span>The orthogonal complement is a subspace of vectors where all of the vectors in it are orthogonal to all of the vectors in a particular subspace. For instance, if you are given a plane in , then the orthogonal complement of that plane is the line that is normal to the plane and that passes through (0,0,0). <br/><br/>The orthogonal component, on the other hand, is a component of a vector. Any vector in  can be written in one unique way as a sum of one vector in the plane and and one vector in the orthogonal complement of the plane. The latter vector is the orthogonal component. <br/><br/>The Gram-Schmidt Process is not the process of getting the orthogonal complement of a subspace from the original subspace. It is actually a method of creating an orthonormal coordinate system. For more information about orthonomal bases, Sal explains it at <a href="https://www.khanacademy.org/math/linear-algebra/alternate_bases/orthonormal_basis/v/linear-algebra-introduction-to-orthonormal-bases" rel="nofollow">https://www.khanacademy.org/math/linear-algebra/alternate_bases/orthonormal_basis/v/linear-algebra-introduction-to-orthonormal-bases</a>. <br/><br/>The 3rd question is not correct, but I'll answer this question anyway. A null space is the set of vectors which when multiplied by a matrix gives the 0 vector.</span></span></div><div class="_u0lfh1z"><div class="_1chv0opk"><button class="_aoepj1"><span Math.<br/>d. Of two vectors or functions: perpendicular; having an inner product [dot product] equal to zero. <br/><br/>- from Greek orthogonios "right-angled," from ortho- "straight" + gonia "angle".<br/><br/>Notice that a zero vector is always orthogonal to any vector in the same vector space (its dot product with any other vector is 0), but not at right angles (perpendicular). (So if 2 vectors are perpendicular, they are orthogonal, but if they are orthogonal, they aren't necessarily perpendicular.)</span></span></div><div class="_u0lfh1z"><div class="_1chv0opk"><button class="_aoepj1"><span class="_g20yn58"><div aria-hidden="true" class="_xu2jcg">Comment</div><div class="_s217esa">Comment on InnocentRealist's post orthogonal:

aria-label="Go to InnocentRealist's user profile." class="_dwmetq" href="/profile/kaid_506633881905949995054710/discussion"><span class="_1juyo7q2">InnocentRealist</span></a></div><a class="_1g8isxy8" href="/math/linear-algebra/alternate-bases/othogonal-complements/v/linear-algebra-orthogonal-complements?qa_expand_key=ag5zfmtoYW4tYWNhZGVteXJpCxIIVXNlckRhdGEiRnVzZXJfaWRfa2V5X2h0dHA6Ly9pZC5raGFuYWNhZGVteS5vcmcvMzZkODZmMmI4NzQ5NDM4OGI0ZjRkMDA1M2MxZmNkNTMMCxIIRmVlZGJhY2sYgICA8PzHkQoM&amp;qa_expand_type=answer"><span class="_1eypurfx"><div aria-hidden="true" class="_xu2jcg">6 years ago</div><div class="_s217esa">Posted 6 years ago. Direct link to InnocentRealist's post I don't really know why h...</div></span></a><div aria-live="polite" class="_s217esa" role="status"></div></div></div><div class="_4ebu80"><span aria-atomic="true" aria-live="polite" aria-relevant="additions text" class="_19bpjuy" data-test-id="dropdown-live-region"></span><button aria-expanded="false" aria-haspopup="menu" class="_19klbmsr" disabled="" type="button"><div class="_xu2jcg"><span class="_133hxe5q">more</span></div><div aria-hidden="true" class="_akfsuo"></div><svg class="_aeiopc" height="16" viewbox="0 0 16 16" width="16"><path d="M8 8.586l3.293-3.293a1 1 0 0 1 1.414 1.414l-4 4a1 1 0 0 1-1.414 0l-4-4a1 1 0 0 1 1.414-1.414L8 8.586z" fill="currentColor"></path></svg></button></div></div><div class="_1nza63bj"><span class="_1glfes6x"><span>I don't really know why he says that. Just pay attention to which vectors are row vectors and which are column vectors. The null space of A is just all the vectors x that satisfy "Ax=0", it's not the transpose of A.  <br/><br/>If a vector "c" is a row vector "[c1 c2 ... cn]" in R^n, then it's a 1xn matrix. If it's a column vector in R^n, then it's a nx1 matrix. If it's just a vector "(c1, c2,..., cn)" in R^n, then it's not a matrix, and it doesn't make sense to transpose it.<br/><br/>The transpose of a (row or column) vector "d" can be either a column or row vector, depending on whether "d" is a row or a column vector. Any (row or column) vector is the transpose of its transpose, and so is a transpose of some other vector. There is no need to call row vectors transposes, as Sal does here, and for me it's confusing (and I think confused) and a distraction. Calling the first row of A "r1" is good enough, because the letter "r" suggests "row vector".</span></span></div><div class="_u0lfh1z"><div class="_1chv0opk"><button class="_aoepj1"><span class="_g20yn58"><div aria-hidden="true" class="_xu2jcg">Comment</div><div class="_s217esa">Comment on InnocentRealist's post I don't really know why h...</div><div class="_s217esa"> <!-- -->Button 15H1.723a1 1 0 0 1-.868-1.496L7.132 2.519a1 1 0 0 1 1.736 0z" fill="rgba(33,36,44,0.64)"></path></svg><span class="_12zdmwuo"><div class="_yva8mme">Upvote<div class="_s217esa"> <!-- -->Button opens as 'V perp', not for 'perpetrator' but for
'perpendicular.' So V perp is equal to the set of
all x's, all the vectors x that are a member of our Rn,
such that x dot V is equal to 0 for every vector V that is
a member of our subspace. So we're essentially saying,
look, you have some subspace, it's got a bunch of
vectors in it. Now if I can find some other
set of vectors where every member of that set is orthogonal
to every member of the subspace in question, then
the set of those vectors is called the orthogonal
complement of V. And you write it this way,
V perp, right there. So the first thing that we just
tend to do when we are defining a space or defining
some set is to see, hey, is this a subspace? Is V perp, or the orthogonal
complement of V, is this a subspace? Well, you might remember from
many, many videos ago, that we had just a couple of conditions
for a subspace. That if-- let's say that a and b
are both a member of V perp, then we have to wonder
whether a plus b is a member of V perp. That's our first condition. It needs to be closed under
addition in order for this to be a subspace. And the next condition as well,
if a is a member of V perp, is some scalar multiple of
a also a member of V perp? And the last one, it has to
contain the zero vector. Which is a little bit redundant
with this, because if any scalar multiple of a is
a member of our orthogonal complement of V, you could
just multiply it by 0. So it would imply that the zero
vector is a member of V. So what does this imply? What is the fact that a and
b are members of V perp? That means that a dot V, where
this V is any member of our original subspace V, is equal
to 0 for any V that is a member of our subspace V. And it also means that b, since
b is also a member of V perp, that V dot any member of
our subspace is also going to be 0, or any b that
is a member of V. So what happens if we
take a plus b dot V? Let's do that. So if I do a plus b dot
V, what is this going to be equal to? This is going to be equal
to a dot V plus b dot V. And we just said, the fact that
both a and b are members of our orthogonal complement
means that both of these quantities are going
to be equal to 0. So this is going to be
equal to 0 plus 0 which is equal to 0. So a plus b is definitely a
member of our orthogonal complement. So we got our check box right
there I'll do it in a different color than

any member of our original subspace this is the same thing

our orthogonal complement, so this is going to
be equal to 0. So this is going to be c times
0, which is equal to 0. So this is also a member
of our orthogonal complement to V. And of course, I can multiply
c times 0 and I would get to 0. Or you could just say, look, 0
is orthogonal to everything. You take the zero vector, dot

going to be a member of any orthogonal complement, because
it obviously is always going to be true for this condition
right here. So we know that V perp, or the
orthogonal complement of V, is a subspace. Which is nice because now we

orthogonal complement is a subspace of vectors where all of the vectors in it are orthogonal to all of the vectors in a particular subspace. For instance, if you are given a plane in , then the orthogonal complement of that plane is the line that is normal to the plane and that passes through (0,0,0). \n\nThe orthogonal component, on the other hand, is a component of a vector. Any vector in  can be written in one unique way as a sum of one vector in the plane and and one vector in the orthogonal complement of the plane. The latter vector is the orthogonal component. \n\nThe Gram-Schmidt Process is not the process of getting the orthogonal complement of a subspace from the original subspace. It is actually a method of creating an orthonormal coordinate system. For more information about orthonomal bases, Sal explains it at https://www.khanacademy.org/math/linear-algebra/alternate_bases/orthonormal_basis/v/linear-algebra-introduction-to-orthonormal-bases. \n\nThe 3rd question is not correct, but I'll answer this question anyway. don't really know why he says that. Just pay attention to which vectors are row vectors and which are column vectors. The null space of A is just all the vectors x that satisfy \"Ax=0\", it's not the transpose of A.  \n\nIf a vector \"c\" is a row vector \"[c1 c2 ... cn]\" in R^n, then it's a 1xn matrix. If it's a column vector in R^n, then it's a nx1 matrix. If it's just a vector \"(c1, c2,..., cn)\" in R^n, then it's not a matrix, and it doesn't make sense to transpose it.\n\nThe transpose of a (row or column) vector \"d\" can be either a column or row vector, depending on whether \"d\" is a row or a column vector. Any (row or column) vector is the transpose of its transpose, and so is a transpose of some other vector. There is no need to call row vectors transposes, as Sal does here, and for me it's confusing (and I think confused) and a distraction. Calling the first row of A \"r1\" is good enough, because the letter \"r\" suggests \"row vector\".","date":"2017-03-08T06:41:47.9298Z","definitelyNotSpam":false,"deleted":false,"downVoted":false,"expandKey":"ag5zfmtoYW4tYWNhZGVteXJpCxIIVXNlckRhdGEiRnVzZXJfaWRfa2V5X2h0dHA6Ly9pZC5raGFuYWNhZGVteS5vcmcvMzZkODZmMmI4NzQ5NDM4OGI0ZjRkMDA1M2MxZmNkNTMMCxIIRmVlZGJhY2sYgICA8PzHkQoM","feedbackType":"ANSWER","flaggedBy":null,"flaggedByUser":false,"flags":null,"focus":{"__typename":"FeedbackFocus","id":"25391","kind":"Video","relativeUrl":"/math/linear-algebra/alternate-bases/othogonal-complements/v/linear-algebra-orthogonal-complements","translatedTitle":"Orthogonal
